                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Rosaceae, with over 100 genera and 3,000 species, is the third most economically important plant family in temperate regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Dirlewanger E. et al. Development of microsatellite markers in peach [ Prunus persica (L.) Batsch] and their use in genetic diversity analysis in peach and sweet cherry ( Prunus avium L.). Theor. Appl. Genet. 105, 127–138 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d46598e753">1</a></sup>. The Rosaceae family has been traditionally divided into four subfamilies according to fruit type: Rosoideae (Rosa, Fragaria, Potentilla and Rubus; fruit, achene; <i>x</i>=7, 8 or 9), Prunoideae (Prunus; fruit, drupe; <i>x</i>=8), Spiraeoideae (Spiraea; fruit, follicle or capsule; <i>x</i>=9) and Maloideae (Malus, Pyrus and Cotoneaster; fruit, pome; <i>x</i>=17)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Potter D., Gao F., Bortiri P. E., Oh S. H. &amp; Baggett S. Phylogenetic relationships in Rosaceae inferred from chloroplast matK and trnL- trnF nucleotide sequence data. Plant Syst. Evol. 231, 77–89 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d46598e770">2</a></sup>.</p><p>The poor phylogenetic resolution of the backbone of the Rosaceae phylogenetic tree suggests rapid evolutionary radiation of lineages within the family, corresponding to a relatively recent divergence of genera<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Potter D. et al. Phylogeny and classification of Rosaceae. Plant Syst. Evol. 266, 5–43 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d46598e777">3</a></sup>. Possibly owing to this rapid evolution, Rosaceae members show significant phenotypic diversity, wherein common morphological synapomorphies are not readily identifiable. Plant habit, chromosome number and fruit type evolved independently within the family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Potter D., Gao F., Bortiri P. E., Oh S. H. &amp; Baggett S. Phylogenetic relationships in Rosaceae inferred from chloroplast matK and trnL- trnF nucleotide sequence data. Plant Syst. Evol. 231, 77–89 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d46598e781">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Potter D. et al. Phylogeny and classification of Rosaceae. Plant Syst. Evol. 266, 5–43 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d46598e784">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Morgan D. R., Soltis D. E. &amp; Robertson K. R. Systematic and evolutionary implications of rbcL sequence variation in Rosaceae. Am. J. Bot. 81, 890–903 (1994)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d46598e787">4</a></sup>. Rosaceous fruits are consumed in multiple forms, including fresh, dried, juice and processed products<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Shulaev V. et al. Multiple models for Rosaceae genomics. Plant Physiol. 147, 985–1003 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d46598e791">5</a></sup>. The economic importance of edible rosaceous crops is attributed to their flavourful fruits and nuts, which provide unique dietary contributions to consumers.</p><p>Several Rosaceae species, including <i>Malus</i> × <i>domestica</i> (apple, 2<i>n</i>=2 × =34)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Velasco R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus x domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d46598e807">6</a></sup>, <i>Fragaria vesca</i> (strawberry, 2<i>n</i>=2 × =14)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Shulaev V. et al. The genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). Nat. Genet. 43, 109–116 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d46598e818">7</a></sup> and <i>Prunus persica</i> (peach, 2<i>n</i>=2 × =16) have already been sequenced. The <i>P. mume</i> (mei, 2<i>n</i>=2 × =16)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Anthony H. et al. The New Royal Horticultural Society Dictionary of Gardening Vol. 3, 738( The Macmillan Press Limited: London and Basingstoke, (1992)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d46598e835">8</a></sup> is a woody perennial tree with a long intergeneration period of 3–5 years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Chen J. Y. Chinese Mei flowers (in Chinese) Vol. 1, 12–58Hainan Publishing House: Haikou, (1996)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d46598e839">9</a></sup>. As the most famous ornamental flower and fruit tree in East Asian countries, <i>P. mume</i> has been domesticated for more than 3,000 years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Chen J. Y. Chinese Mei flowers (in Chinese) Vol. 1, 12–58Hainan Publishing House: Haikou, (1996)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d46598e846">9</a></sup>. In East Asia, <i>P. mume</i>’s flower and young fruit can tolerate low temperatures (−4 to −2 °C) in early spring<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Chen J. Y. Chinese Mei flowers (in Chinese) Vol. 1, 12–58Hainan Publishing House: Haikou, (1996)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d46598e853">9</a></sup>. <i>P. mume</i> blooms in late winter or early spring (typically late January or February) before the new leaves grow.</p><p>The <i>P. mume</i> genome can be used to understand the structure of Rosaceae genomes in general and the rapid evolution of the Rosaceae genus. It will also help to measure and characterize the genetic diversity in domesticated and wild populations of fruit trees and determine how this diversity relates to the tremendous phenotypic diversity in fruit trees. Furthermore, genome-based tools can be developed to improve breeding work.</p><p>Here, using the <i>P. mume</i> genomic sequence and the available <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i> and <i>F. vesca</i> genomic data, we reconstructed the ancestral genome of Rosaceae and analysed the evolution of the three chief genera Prunus, Malus and Fragaria. Our results were consistent with previous studies suggesting that small inversions had a significant function in the evolution of Rosaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Illa E. et al. Comparative analysis of rosaceous genomes and the reconstruction of a putative ancestral genome for the family. BMC Evol. Biol. 11, 9 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d46598e883">10</a></sup>. We identified 1,154 candidate genes that are critical to valuable traits, including flowering scent, flowering time and disease resistance. These genes, with the <i>P. mume</i> population material and the genetic markers that we developed, will be valuable for <i>P. mume</i> breeding and other Rosaceae species studies.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing and assembly</h3><p>Constructing the reference genome for fruit trees is more challenging compared with other plants. The high heterozygosity of fruit trees, caused by artificial grafting and asexual reproduction, imposes technical challenges for the assembly algorithms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Velasco R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus x domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d46598e906">6</a></sup>. The relatively long generation time and difficulties with the population materials impede construction of the genetic map. Further, the rapid evolution in Rosaceae prevents building chromosome structures according to collinearity.</p><p>In order to construct the reference genome of <i>P. mume</i>, we initially sequenced two domesticated <i>P. mume</i> samples (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Methods</a>) using the Illumina Genome Analyser (GA) II. Both samples had a high heterozygous rate, as estimated using K-mer statistics (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Fig. S1a and S1b</a>), and the <i>de novo</i> assembly results by current next-generation sequencing (NGS) algorithms did not meet the reference quality standards (data not shown).</p><p>We then considered wild samples, owing to their lack of artificial grafting and asexual reproduction. We collected wild samples of <i>P. mume</i> in Tongmai, Bomi county, Tibet, China (30°06′N, 95°05′E, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Fig. S2</a>), which is the western end of the region from which domesticated <i>P. mume</i> originated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Bao M. Z. C. J.Y. studies on the variation and distribution of Prunus mume Sieb. et Zucc. Acta Hort. Sin. 21, 81–86 (1994)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d46598e944">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Xing Z., Suo L. Q. P., Liu H. &amp; Zhang Q. X. The germplasm resources survey of Prunus mume at Tangmai (in Chinese). Northern Horticulture 10, 129–132 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d46598e947">12</a></sup>. In a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Chen J. Y. Chinese Mei flowers (in Chinese) Vol. 1, 12–58Hainan Publishing House: Haikou, (1996)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d46598e951">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Bao M. Z. C. J.Y. studies on the variation and distribution of Prunus mume Sieb. et Zucc. Acta Hort. Sin. 21, 81–86 (1994)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d46598e954">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Xing Z., Suo L. Q. P., Liu H. &amp; Zhang Q. X. The germplasm resources survey of Prunus mume at Tangmai (in Chinese). Northern Horticulture 10, 129–132 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d46598e957">12</a></sup>, we determined that the origin of <i>P. mume</i> was confined to an area of ~0.7 km<sup>2</sup> and that the primary distribution area was &lt;0.3 km<sup>2</sup> below an altitude of 2,230 m. Samples from this region were highly homozygous owing to generations of self-fertilization in a hermetic geographic environment. We chose one such wild sample to be sequenced, and its low heterozygosity was confirmed by sequencing and K-mer statistics (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Fig. S1c</a>).</p><p>We generated 50.4 Gb of sequencing data for this wild sample using the Illumina GA II, including three short-insert (180–800 bp) pair-end libraries and five large-insert (2–40 Kb) mate-pair libraries (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S2</a>). SOAP<i>denovo</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Li R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d46598e980">13</a></sup>, a genome assembler algorithm that is based on <i>de Bruijn</i> graph, was used to assemble the <i>P. mume</i> genome. Based on K-mer statistics, the <i>P. mume</i> genome was estimated to be 280 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Fig. S1c</a>). Approximately 84.6% (237 Mb) of its genome was assembled. The contig N50 of the assembled sequence was 31.8 Kb (longest, 201.1 Kb), and scaffold N50 was 577.8 Kb (longest, 2.87 Mb) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#Tab1">Table 1</a>). By mapping raw reads back to the draft genome, we observed a heterozygosity rate of 0.03% in this wild sample (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S3</a>), supporting our assumption of low heterozygosity in the wild sample. We identified 125,383 bp and 19,897 bp sequences in assembly similar to chloroplast and mitochondrial sequences, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S4</a>). Although most of the regions were shorter than 1,000 bp, we found a ~25,000 kb chloroplast-similar region, which might be the plasmid sequence or plastid-transferred nuclear fragment.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of <i>P. mume</i> genome assembly.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ncomms2290/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To improve the assembly of the <i>P. mume</i> genome, we performed whole-genome mapping (WGM), an automated high-throughput optical mapping method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Zhou S. et al. Single-molecule approach to bacterial genomic comparisons via optical mapping. J. Bacteriol. 186, 7773–7782 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d46598e1470">14</a></sup>, to generate an entire genomic map (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). We constructed the WGM map with 300-fold whole-genome depth using BamHI and NheI independently. Through an iterative assembly strategy, combined with WGM and sequence data, 170 scaffolds that were assembled by NGS were grouped into 49 large scaffolds, thus the scaffold N50 improved significantly from 578 Kb to 1.1 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#Tab1">Table 1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="WGM assembly and anchoring of P. mume genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: WGM assembly and anchoring of <i>P. mume</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2290/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2290/MediaObjects/41467_2012_Article_BFncomms2290_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2290/MediaObjects/41467_2012_Article_BFncomms2290_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="986" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Assembly of <i>P. mume</i> genome by WGM. (<b>b</b>) Anchoring the <i>P. mume</i> genome into eight linkage groups using 779 high-quality SNP markers. Yellow scaffolds were anchored by WGM and SNP markers, whereas the blue scaffolds were anchored by SNP markers.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2290/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Then we constructed a high-density genetic map by applying restriction-site-associated DNA (RAD) marker strategy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Chutimanitsakun Y. et al. Construction and application for QTL analysis of a restriction site associated DNA (RAD) linkage map in barley. BMC Genomics 12, 4 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d46598e1519">15</a></sup> in a segregating F<sub>1</sub> population. The consensus genetic map contained eight linkage groups, consisted of a set of 1,484 high-quality single-nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) markers (co-dominate markers), 779 of which were used in anchoring and orienting scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>). The genetic map improves the quality of the reference and will be useful in molecular marker-assisted breeding and further map-based cloning.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome annotation</h3><p>We annotated 106.8 Mb (45.0% of the assembled genome) of repetitive sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S5</a>) in the <i>P. mume</i> genome by integrating the <i>de novo</i> and homologue-based approaches. Transposable elements (TEs) were the predominant components, constituting 97.9% of all repetitive sequences. The long terminal repeat <i>Copia</i> family and <i>Gypsy</i> family were the most abundant TEs in the <i>P. mume</i> genome. The TE divergence rate suggested a lack of recent amplification (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Fig. S3</a>). These were conserved in <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i> and <i>F. vesca</i> while the <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i> have a much higher proportion in <i>Gypsy</i> family than <i>P. mume</i> and <i>F. vesca</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S6</a>).</p><p>To improve the gene annotation, we generated 11.3 Gb of RNA-Seq data from five major tissues: bud, fruit, leaf, root and stem (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S7</a>). Using these data, integrated with <i>ab initio</i> homologue prediction methods (Methods), we annotated 31,390 protein-coding genes, comparable with the value in <i>F. vesca</i> (34,809)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Shulaev V. et al. The genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). Nat. Genet. 43, 109–116 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d46598e1598">7</a></sup> and less than in <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i> (57,386)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Velasco R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus x domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d46598e1609">6</a></sup>, as expected. The transcript length in <i>P. mume</i> was 2,514 bp on average, with 4.6 exons per gene (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S8</a>). Approximately 82.5% of all genes could be functionally annotated following a consensus method of either known homologous or predictive sequence signatures using Swissprot, GO, TrEMBL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Bairoch A. &amp; Apweiler R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 45–48 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d46598e1619">16</a></sup>, InterPro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Zdobnov E. M. &amp; Apweiler R. InterProScan--an integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics 17, 847–848 (2001)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d46598e1623">17</a></sup> or KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Kanehisa M. &amp; Goto S. KEGG: kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 27–30 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d46598e1628">18</a></sup>. About 98.3% of these annotations were not hypothetical or uncharacterized, and we considered them as high confidence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S9</a>). We noted that 25,854 (82.6%) gene models were expressed, of which 768, 308, 240, 762 and 179 genes were expressed solely in bud, fruit, leaf, root and stem, respectively. We further integrated 82,832 peach expressed sequence tag (EST) sequences, together with RNAseq data, 85.1% of gene models represented as transcripts. We also annotated the non-coding RNA genes in the current assembly including 508 transfer RNA, 209 microRNA, 125 ribosomal RNA and 287 small nuclear RNA (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S10</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome evolution</h3><p>By genome self-alignment by MCscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Tang H. et al. Unraveling ancient hexaploidy through multiply-aligned angiosperm gene maps. Genome Res. 18, 1944–1954 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d46598e1646">19</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Fig. S4</a>, Method) and the number of transversions at fourfold degenerate sites (4DTv) distribution of duplicated pairs, there was no recent whole-genome duplication (WGD) after species differentiation between <i>P. mume</i> and <i>M.</i> × <i>demestica</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="The evolution of P. mume."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: The evolution of <i>P. mume</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2290/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2290/MediaObjects/41467_2012_Article_BFncomms2290_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2290/MediaObjects/41467_2012_Article_BFncomms2290_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1261" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) 4DTv distribution of duplicate gene pairs in <i>P. mume</i> and <i>M.</i> × <i>domestic</i>a, calculated based on alignment of codons with the HKY (Hasegawa, Kishino and Yano) substitution model. (<b>b</b>) The duplication of <i>P. mume</i> by paralogous pairs in the <i>P. mume</i> genome (chromosomes Chr1–Chr8). Each line represents a duplicated gene. The seven colours reflect the seven ancestral eudicot linkage groups (A1, A4, A7, A10, A13, A16 and A19).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2290/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We examined the paleo-history in Prunus and found that there was a triplicated arrangement (ancestral γ event). We aligned 27,819 gene models to the seven paleo-hexaploid ancestor chromosome groups in grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Jaillon O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d46598e1715">20</a></sup> and identified that the colinearity blocks contained 2,772 orthologs, the extent of these blocks covered 78.1% of the <i>P. mume</i> genome.</p><p>Further, we aligned 27,819 <i>P. mume</i> gene models to themselves and identified seven major blocks of duplication, which corresponded to 194 gene pairs, covering 38.5% of the anchored genome. The chromosome-to-chromosome relationships P5–P7, P2–P4–P8, P1–P2–P4–P6, P1–P5, P2–P6–P8, P2–P4–P7 and P3–P4 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S11</a>) suggested that triplicated arrangement (γ event) marks remained in the <i>P. mume</i> genome. Based on evidence of paleo-hexaploidization (γ event) and lineage-specific duplications in eudicots, it was possible to examine chromosomal changes during the evolution of <i>P. mume</i> and other Rosaceae species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Reconstruction of ancestral chromosomes of Rosaceae</h3><p>We reconstructed nine ancestral chromosomes of the Rosaceae family and determined the history of chromosome fusion, fission and duplication in the three major Rosaceae subfamilies. Previous studies have reported the eudicots ancestor with seven proto-chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Jaillon O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d46598e1748">20</a></sup> and the grass ancestor with five proto-chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Salse J. et al. Identification and characterization of shared duplications between rice and wheat provide new insight into grass genome evolution. Plant Cell 20, 11–24 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d46598e1752">21</a></sup>. Using <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i> as a reference, we analysed the syntenic relationships among the <i>P. mume</i> (P), <i>F. vesca</i> (F) and <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i> (M).</p><p>One hundred and fifty-one blocks that contained 4,546 orthologous genes, covering 96.9% of the anchored <i>P. mume</i> genome, were identified between <i>P. mume</i> and <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i> versus 132 blocks with 2,031 orthologous genes, covering 88.8% of the <i>F. vesca</i> genome, between <i>F. vesca</i> and <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i>. The chromosome-to-chromosome orthologous relationships were shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Fig. S5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S12</a>.</p><p>Combining the intergenomic and intragenomic analysis of the Rosaceae genomes, we noted the following primary chromosome pair combinations: M5–M10/P3–P6/F2–F3, M3–M11/P1–P3–P6/F2–F3–F5, M9–M17/P2–P4/F1–F6, M13–M16/P2/F4, M4–M12–M14/P1–P2–P6–P8/F5–F6, M6–M14/P7/F5, M1–M2–M7/P5/F7, M8–M15/P2/F2–F5 and M1–M2–M15/P1–P2–P3–P8/F1 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Fig. S5</a>). We reconstructed a putative ancestral genome of Rosaceae and proposed an evolutionary scenario of <i>P. mume</i>, <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i> and <i>F. vesca</i> from the putative 9-chromosome ancestor (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Evolutionary model of Rosaceae genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Evolutionary model of Rosaceae genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2290/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2290/MediaObjects/41467_2012_Article_BFncomms2290_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2290/MediaObjects/41467_2012_Article_BFncomms2290_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="659" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The Rosaceae ancestor chromosomes are represented by nine colours. The various evolutionary processes from the common ancestor are indicated as R (WGD) and F (fusions of chromosomes). In the second layer, different colours in each chromosome represent origin from the common ancestral chromosomes. The current structure of the Rosaceae genomes is shown at the bottom of the figure. In some regions, we were not able to determine which ancestral chromosome they came from, and those regions were represented as white spaces.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2290/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In <i>P. mume</i>, chromosomes 4, 5 and 7 did not undergo rearrangement, coming directly from ancient chromosomes III, VII and VI, respectively. Chromosome 1 came from ancestral chromosome II, V and IX. Chromosome 2 originated from ancient chromosome IV and VIII into which some of chromosomes III and V were inserted. Chromosome 3 came from chromosomes I, II and IX; chromosome 6 came from I, II and V; and chromosome 8 originated from ancient chromosomes V and IX. Thus, we hypothesized that at least 11 fissions and 11 fusions occurred in <i>P. mume</i> from the nine common ancestral chromosomes. For <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i>, at least one WGD and five fusions took place to reach the 17-chromosome structure, compared with 15 fusions for <i>F. vesca</i> to effect the 7-chromosome structure.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Early blooming of <i>P. mume</i></h3><p><i>P. mume</i> is one of the first trees that bloom in early spring, blooming even below 0 °C. Thus, <i>P. mume</i> has a system to acclimate to cold weather and release itself from dormancy. The dormancy-associated MADS-box transcription factors (DAM) family, which is related to dormancy induction and release<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Sasaki R. et al. Functional and expressional analyses of PmDAM genes associated with endodormancy in Japanese apricot. Plant Physiol. 157, 485–497 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d46598e1885">22</a></sup>, was identified in the <i>P. mume</i> assembly, and all six DAM genes were noted in the tandem array (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S13</a>). Sasaki <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Sasaki R. et al. Functional and expressional analyses of PmDAM genes associated with endodormancy in Japanese apricot. Plant Physiol. 157, 485–497 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d46598e1901">22</a></sup> identified six DAM genes, all of which were transcriptional repressors in <i>P. mume</i>. They also found that all DAM genes were repressed during prolonged exposure to cold and maintained at low levels until endodormancy release. Jimenez<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Jimenez S., Reighard G. L. &amp; Bielenberg D. G. Gene expression of DAM5 and DAM6 is suppressed by chilling temperatures and inversely correlated with bud break rate. Plant Mol. Biol. 73, 157–167 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d46598e1908">23</a></sup> observed that the expression of DAM3, DAM5 and DAM6 was suppressed by cool temperatures, bottoming on bud break in <i>P. persica</i>. These findings suggest that the DAM genes inhibit dormancy release and their expression are suppressed during prolonged exposure to cold to allow bud release from dormancy.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Six tandemly arrayed DAM genes in P. mume."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Six tandemly arrayed DAM genes in <i>P. mume</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2290/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2290/MediaObjects/41467_2012_Article_BFncomms2290_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms2290/MediaObjects/41467_2012_Article_BFncomms2290_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="433" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Overview of PmDAM in the <i>P. mume</i> genome. Six PmDAM genes are located as tandem repeats. (<b>b</b>) Structures of PmDAM genes in <i>P. mume</i>. Boxes and lines represent exons and introns, whereas red points represent CBF-binding site.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms2290/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We hypothesized that the DAM genes explained the early dormancy release of <i>P. mume</i>. To this end, we examined the phylogenetic relationships among DAM genes in <i>P. mume</i> using the PHYML 3.0 software. The molecular evolution models of the six tandem DAM genes in <i>P. mume</i> suggested that they were derived from serial duplication events in the following order: PmDAM1, PmDAM3, PmDAM2, PmDAM5, PmDAM4 and PmDAM6 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Fig. S6</a>). This model was consistent with previous studies in the peach genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Jimenez S., Lawton-Rauh A. L., Reighard G. L., Abbott A. G. &amp; Bielenberg D. G. Phylogenetic analysis and molecular evolution of the dormancy associated MADS-box genes from peach. BMC Plant Biol. 9, 81 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d46598e1966">24</a></sup>, and we suggest that these duplication events are unique in the Prunus subfamily—we did not find these tandem DAM genes in <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i> or <i>F. vesca</i>. Two other programs, MEGA 4 (ref.<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Tamura K., Dudley J., Nei M. &amp; Kumar S. MEGA4: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis (MEGA) software version 4.0. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1596–1599 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d46598e1979">25</a>) and PAML4 (ref.<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Yang Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d46598e1982">26</a>), obtained similar results. By estimation of pairwise dN and dS rates using MEGA 4, there was significant purifying selection and no significant positive selection of the six sequences. In an analysis of <i>P. mume</i> DAM genes, most sites were highly conserved, with a dN/dS rate ratio near 0, or nearly neutral.</p><p>In addition to DAM, C-repeat-binding transcription factors (CBF) mediate the establishment of early dormancy release—overexpression of PpCBF1 in <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i> results in strong sensitivity to short day lengths and induced dormancy at optimal growth temperatures<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Wisniewski M., Norelli J., Bassett C., Artlip T. &amp; Macarisin D. Ectopic expression of a novel peach (Prunus persica) CBF transcription factor in apple (Malus x domestica) results in short-day induced dormancy and increased cold hardiness. Planta 233, 971–983 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d46598e1998">27</a></sup>. Horvath <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Horvath D. P., Sung S., Kim D., Chao W. &amp; Anderson J. Characterization, expression and function of DORMANCY ASSOCIATED MADS-BOX genes from leafy spurge. Plant Mol. Biol. 73, 169–179 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2004">28</a></sup> noted that EeDAM1 was cold stress-responsive and contained putative CBF-binding sites, which were <i>cis</i>-regulating motifs that were targeted by the cold/drought stress CBF regulon in the 2,000-bp region upstream of the EeDAM1 translation start codon. This finding suggests that CBF control the cold-responsive <i>EeDAM1</i> gene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Horvath D. P., Sung S., Kim D., Chao W. &amp; Anderson J. Characterization, expression and function of DORMANCY ASSOCIATED MADS-BOX genes from leafy spurge. Plant Mol. Biol. 73, 169–179 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2015">28</a></sup>. Similar to EeDAM1, conserved CBF sites were identified in the 1,000-bp region upstream of the translation start codons in DAM4–DAM6 in peach and Japanese apricot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Sasaki R. et al. Functional and expressional analyses of PmDAM genes associated with endodormancy in Japanese apricot. Plant Physiol. 157, 485–497 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2019">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Jimenez S., Lawton-Rauh A. L., Reighard G. L., Abbott A. G. &amp; Bielenberg D. G. Phylogenetic analysis and molecular evolution of the dormancy associated MADS-box genes from peach. BMC Plant Biol. 9, 81 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2022">24</a></sup>. In the <i>P. mume</i> genome, we identified 13 CBF orthologous genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S14</a>) and 7 CBF regulons, late embryogenesis-abundant proteins/dehydrins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Vogel J. T., Zarka D. G., Van Buskirk H. A., Fowler S. G. &amp; Thomashow M. F. Roles of the CBF2 and ZAT12 transcription factors in configuring the low temperature transcriptome of Arabidopsis. Plant J. 41, 195–211 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2032">29</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S15</a>). In the upstream regions of DAM genes in <i>P. mume</i>, we noted more putative CBF-binding sites on DAM4, DAM5 and DAM6 than in peach, and found novel sites, one on DAM1 and two on DAM6 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>). We suggest that these additional sites render <i>P. mume</i> more sensitive to cold and result in early blooming in spring. In summary, our analyses have increased our understanding of the molecular control of dormancy, flowering regulation and acclimation to cold. The CBF and DAM genes that we identified in <i>P. mume</i> might allow molecular biology facilities to breed ornamental plants and fruits with disparate blooming times.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Disease resistance-related genes and pathogenic interaction</h3><p>The search for natural disease resistance is a chief objective in <i>P. mume</i> breeding. In the <i>P. mume</i> genome, we identified 253 leucine-rich repeat receptor-like kinase (LRR-RLK) genes, comparable with the number in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Shiu S. H. &amp; Bleecker A. B. Receptor-like kinases from Arabidopsis form a monophyletic gene family related to animal receptor kinases. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 98, 10763–10768 (2001)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2068">30</a></sup> (221) and <i>Theobroma cacao</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Argout X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101–108 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2074">31</a></sup> (253) and less than in <i>Populus trichocarpa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Kohler A. et al. Genome-wide identification of NBS resistance genes in Populus trichocarpa. Plant Mol. Biol. 66, 619–636 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2081">32</a></sup> (436) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S16</a>). The LRR-XI and LRR-XII subfamilies expanded tremendously in the <i>P. mume</i> genome compared with <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Shiu S. H. &amp; Bleecker A. B. Receptor-like kinases from Arabidopsis form a monophyletic gene family related to animal receptor kinases. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 98, 10763–10768 (2001)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2093">30</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S17</a>); similar expansions have been observed in <i>T. cacao</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Argout X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101–108 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2103">31</a></sup> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Kohler A. et al. Genome-wide identification of NBS resistance genes in Populus trichocarpa. Plant Mol. Biol. 66, 619–636 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2109">32</a></sup>. The nucleotide-binding site-coding resistant gene (NBS-coding R gene) is one of the largest families in <i>P. mume</i>, with 411 members, more than twice that of <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Meyers B. C., Kozik A., Griego A., Kuang H. &amp; Michelmore R. W. Genome-wide analysis of NBS-LRR-encoding genes in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 15, 809–834 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2118">33</a></sup> and <i>F. vesca</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Shulaev V. et al. The genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). Nat. Genet. 43, 109–116 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2125">7</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S18</a>).</p><p>Pathogenesis-related (PR) proteins are plant-coded proteins are induced by various stress stimuli. PR proteins mediate plant defence against pathogenic constraints and general adaptation to stressful environments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Liu J.-J. &amp; Ekramoddoullah A. K. M. The family 10 of plant pathogenesis-related proteins: their structure, regulation, and function in response to biotic and abiotic stresses. Physiol. Mol. Plant Path. 68, 3–13 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2135">34</a></sup>. Most PR gene families in <i>P. mume</i> were expanded notably and were arranged in tandem, especially PR10 (22 of 25) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S19</a>). By genomic analysis, the majority of PR10 members in <i>P. mume</i> formed a cluster of &lt;100 Kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S20</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Fig. S7</a>), but we did not find this cluster in the other sequenced Rosacea genomes. Further, the PR10 families encode hypothetical proteins that are highly expressed at the transcriptional level in roots and leaves in <i>P. mume</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Fig. S8</a>). Thus, the expansion of the PR10 family might be related to the response of <i>P. mume</i> to salt, drought and fungal infection in the roots and leaves<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Moons A., Prinsen E., Bauw G. &amp; Van Montagu M. Antagonistic effects of abscisic acid and jasmonates on salt stress-inducible transcripts in rice roots. Plant Cell 9, 2243–2259 (1997)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2164">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Pinto M. P. &amp; Ricardo C. P. Lupinus albus L. pathogenesis-related proteins that show similarity to PR-10 proteins. Plant Physiol. 109, 1345–1351 (1995)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2167">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Koistinen K. M. et al. Birch PR-10c is induced by factors causing oxidative stress but appears not to confer tolerance to these agents. New Phytol. 155, 381–391 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2170">37</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Floral scent</h3><p>Floral scent, determined by a complex mixture of low-molecular-weight volatile molecules, has significant function in the reproductive processes of many plants and enhances the aesthetic properties of ornamental plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Pichersky E. &amp; Dudareva N. Scent engineering: toward the goal of controlling how flowers smell. Trends Biotechnol. 25, 105–110 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2182">38</a></sup>. In earlier research, we determined that the dominant compound classes in <i>P. mume</i> flowers were benzenoid/phenylpropanoid from the cinnamic acid pathway and that terpene compounds were also present.</p><p>Genes related to the biosynthesis of volatile compounds, such as benzyl alcohol acetyltransferase (BEAT), which catalyses the synthesis of benzyl acetate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Aranovich D., Lewinsohn E. &amp; Zaccai M. Post-harvest enhancement of aroma in transgenic lisianthus (Eustoma grandiflorum) using the Clarkia breweri benzyl alcohol acetyltransferase (BEAT) gene. Postharvest Biol. Technol. 43, 255–260 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2192">39</a></sup>, were identified in the <i>P. mume</i> genome. The BEAT gene family expanded notably in <i>P. mume</i> (34 members) compared with <i>Malus</i> × <i>domestics</i> (16), <i>F. vesca</i> (14), <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (4), <i>P. trichocarpa</i> (17) and <i>A. thaliana</i> (3) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S21</a>). Twenty-six of 34 <i>P. mume</i> BEAT genes lay in clusters, the largest of which contained 12 members that were arranged in tandem (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Fig. S9</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms2290#MOESM1521">Supplementary Table S22</a>), suggesting that BEAT genes originated from serial duplication events, unlike the other sequenced plants. In summary, the expansion of the BEAT gene family might increase the content of benzyl acetate and be related to the special fragrance of <i>P. mume.</i> Research on the <i>P. mume</i> genome should allow us to breed novel aromatic cultivars and other aromatic plants in the Rosaceae family.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>We are eager to establish an appropriate reference genome in Rosids species, which includes one-third of all flowering plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Hummer K. E., Janick J. In Rosaceae: Taxonomy, Economic Importance, Genomics Genetics and Genomics of Rosaceae Vol 6, eds Folta K. M., Gardiner S. E. 1–17 (Springer: New York, 2009)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2249">40</a></sup>. Currently, draft genome sequences are available for genome-level comparisons and analysis in three model Rosaceae species—<i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i>, <i>F. vesca</i> and <i>P. mume</i>. The domesticated apple (<i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i>), the main fruit crop of temperate regions throughout the world, is highly heterozygous and has a large genome, which creates technical challenges in assembling its genome, resulting in 1,629 metacontigs.</p><p>The strawberry (<i>F. vesca</i>) has a much smaller genome of ~240 Mb, allowing functional gene studies within Rosaceae. Although the strawberry is useful for functional genomics research, most related high-value fruit plants in the Rosaceae species—peach (<i>P. persica</i>), pear (<i>Pyrus nivalis</i>) and cherry (<i>Prunus avium</i>)—are woody plants, not herbaceous. Unfortunately, the nearest relatives of woody fruit crops usually have a cumbersome polyploid genome.</p><p>After a detailed study of its origin and the current distribution in <i>P. mume</i>, we obtained a suitable sample from an isolated group in the area origin of <i>P. mume</i> for genomic sequencing and assembly. We successfully assembled the genomic sequence of <i>P. mume</i> by NGS data owing to its small genome of ~280 Mb and low heterozygosity. With WGM, we increased the scaffold quality to 1.1 Mb in N50 and constructed <i>P. mume</i> pseudochromosomes using 779 SNP markers from eight linkage groups. We conclude that the combination of Illumina GA, WGM technologies and the genetic map constructed by RAD can be used to perform <i>de novo</i> sequencing of plant genomes, allowing high-quality, rapid and low-cost sequencing of other plant species with similar genomic composition.</p><p>The sequence of the <i>P. mume</i> genome is a valuable resource for biological research and breeding. Based on the sequences of <i>P. mume</i>, <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i> and <i>F. vesca</i>, we reconstructed nine ancestral chromosomes of the Rosaceae family and inferred that they were shaped from an ancestor. The genome also increases our knowledge of the evolution of the Rosaceae. The analysis of the <i>P. mume</i> genome and transcriptome can provide insights into the mechanisms of flowering scent, flowering dormancy and disease resistance.</p><p><i>Note added in proof</i>: A paper describing in detail the full protocols used for the optical whole genome mapping has recently been published<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Dong Y. et al. Sequencing and automated whole-genome optical mapping of the genome of a domestic goat (Capra hircus). Nat. Biotechnol doi:10.1038/nbt.2478 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2333">52</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">DNA preparation and whole-genome shotgun sequencing</h3><p>We used a whole-genome shotgun sequencing strategy with the Illumina GA. Total DNA was extracted from fresh young leaves of the wild species in Tongmai town, Tibet, China (accession No. BJFU1210120008), with the plant genomic DNA extraction Kit (Tiangen, Beijing, China) following the manufacturer’s instructions. To obtain enough DNA to construct and sequence the library, we performed whole-genome amplification. We constructed eight paired-end sequencing libraries with insert sizes of ~180, 500, 800 bp, 2, 5, 10, 20 and 40 kb. For small-insert libraries, 5 μg of DNA were sheared to fragments of 180–800 bp, end-repaired, A-tailed and ligated to Illumina paired-end adapters (Illumina). The ligated fragments were size selected at 180, 500 and 800 bp on agarose gel and amplified by ligation-mediated PCR to produce the corresponding short-insert libraries. For long-insert size library construction, 20–40 μg of genomic DNA were sheared to the desired insert size using nebulization for 2 kb or HydroShear (Covaris) for 5, 10, 20 and 40 kb. Next, the DNA fragments were end-repaired using biotinylated nucleotide analogues, size selected at 2, 5, 10, 20 and 40 kb and circularized by intramolecular ligation. Circular DNA molecules were sheared with Adaptive Focused Acoustic (Covaris) to an average size of 500 bp. Biotinylated fragments were purified with magnetic beads (Invitrogen, CA, USA), end-repaired, A-tailed and ligated to Illumina paired-end adapters, size-selected again and purified by ligation-mediated PCR. In total, we generated 50.4 Gb data of paired ends (PEs), ranging from 50 to 100 bp. These data were checked and filtered on the reads that were generated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Li R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2349">13</a></sup>. We filtered the low-quality reads with following filtering criteria: reads had an ‘N’ over 10% of their length; reads contained more than 40-bp low-quality bases (quality score ≤5); reads contained more than 10-bp adapter sequences (allowing ≤2 bp mismatches); small-insert size paired-end reads that were overlapped (≥10 bp); and Read1 and Read2 of paired-end reads were completely identical that meant these paired-end reads were determined to be artefacts of PCR experiment. Using stringent criteria, 28.4-Gb high-quality data were used for <i>de novo</i> genome assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genome assembly</h3><p>We performed whole-genome assembly using SOAP<i>denovo</i> (Version 1.05)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Li R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2367">13</a></sup> with high-quality reads. SOAP<i>denovo</i> loaded reads into computer memory and constructed de Bruijn graph. The graph was simplified, broke the connections at repeat boundaries and output the unambiguous sequence fragments as contigs. After realigned all usable reads to the contig sequences and obtained aligned PEs, SOAPdenove calculated the number of shared PEs relationships between each pair of contigs, weighed the rate of consistent and conflicting PEs, and constructed the scaffolds step by step. To close the gaps inside the constructed scaffolds, which were composed primarily of repeats that were masked before scaffold construction, we used the PEs information to retrieve the read pairs that had one end mapped to the unique contig and the other located in the gap regions and performed local assembly using GapCloser (v1.12, <a href="http://soap.genomics.org.cn/down/GapCloser_release_2011.tar.gz">http://soap.genomics.org.cn/down/GapCloser_release_2011.tar.gz</a>) with these collected reads.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Whole-genome mapping</h3><p>High-quality DNA (high molecular weight, &gt;200 kb) that was specific for WGM was prepared from fresh <i>P. mume</i> leaves. Whole-genome shotgun single-molecule restriction maps were generated with the automated Argus system (OpGen Inc., MD, USA). First, DNA molecules were deposited onto silane-derivatized glass surfaces in MapCards (OpGen Inc.) and digested with <i>Nhe</i>I or <i>Bam</i>HI for 20 min. Then, the DNA was stained with JOJO fluorescent dye (Invitrogen) and imaged with the Argus system. Overall, 243,174 single-molecule restriction maps (&gt;250 kb) with an average size of 344 kb were generated. The total size was ~83.6 Gb.</p><p>When using WGM data to extend scaffolds, the original scaffold sequences were first digested <i>in silico</i> to generate corresponding restriction maps for each scaffold. Then, the <i>in silico</i> restriction maps were used as seeds to identify single molecules by length-based alignment using the Genome-Builder<sup>TM</sup> software package ( <a href="http://www.opgen.com/products-and-services/softwares/genome-builder">http://www.opgen.com/products-and-services/softwares/genome-builder</a>). These single-molecule maps were assembled together to extend the scaffolds with consensus restriction maps. Meanwhile, the scaffolds with low-coverage regions at both ends were trimmed, and only the high-quality extensions remained.</p><p>To extend sufficient scaffolds, this alignment–assembly process was iterated four times. All pairwise alignments were considered initial candidates for scaffold connection. The alignments with the highest scores remained when conflicts occurred. Super-scaffolds were constructed using the adjacent overlapping relationships between scaffolds; simultaneously, the orientation between each pair of scaffolds could be determined. The details on scaffold alignment and orientation can be obtained from the manufacturer of OpGen.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Genetic map construction and scaffold anchoring</h3><p>A total of 260 F<sub>1</sub> seedlings of the cross between ‘Fenban’ and ‘Kouzi Yudie’ were used to construct the linkage map. The linkage analysis was performed using JoinMap, version 3.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Van Ooijen J. W. &amp; Voorrips R. E. JoinMap 3.0: Software for the calculation of genetic linkage maps. Plant Research International Wageningen, The Netherlands1–51 (2001)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2428">41</a>). RAD-based SNP markers were first tested against the expected segregation ratio. Two heterozygous SNP alleles between two parents were expected to segregate at a 1:2:1 ratio. One heterozygous and one homozygous SNP allele between two parents were expected to segregate at a 1:1 ratio. Distorted markers (<i>P</i>&lt;0.01) were filtered to construct a genetic map by <i>χ</i><sup>2</sup> test. Then, reads that contained SNP markers were aligned to the scaffolds. Only unique aligned SNPs with a cutoff of 87.5% identity remained, per BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Kent W. J. BLAT--the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2439">42</a></sup> (coverage ≥0.90). An LOD score of 12.0 was initially set as the linkage threshold for linkage group identification. Eight linkage groups that had the same number of <i>P. mume</i> chromosomes were formed at an LOD threshold of 12.0. All SNP markers were used to construct the <i>P. mume</i> consensus map with the CP population model in JoinMap, version 3.0.</p><p>To reduce the complex of scaffolds that were anchored to hundreds of SNP markers, a tag SNP was selected from each scaffold with multiple SNPs. We calculated the recombination fractions between all pairs of SNPs in a scaffold and chose the SNP that had the minimum recombination fraction in the sum. Tag SNPs were used to identify the order of scaffolds. Then, two marginal SNPs were used to orient the scaffolds. Scaffolds with only one SNP marker could be anchored but not oriented owing to a lack of markers. One hundred scaffolds (18% of 567 scaffolds) were labelled ‘uncertain orientation.’</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Gene prediction</h3><p>To predict genes, four approaches were used: <i>de novo</i> prediction, homology-based method, EST-based method and transcript-to-genome sequences. For <i>de novo</i> prediction, Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Stanke M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W435–W439 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2468">43</a></sup>, GENSCAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Salamov A. A. &amp; Solovyev V. V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516–522 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2472">44</a></sup> and GlimmerHMM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Majoros W. H., Pertea M. &amp; Salzberg S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878–2879 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2476">45</a></sup> were used with parameters trained on <i>A. thaliana</i>. For the homology search, we mapped the protein sequences of four sequenced plants (<i>Cucumis sativus</i>, <i>Carica papaya</i>, <i>F. vesca</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>) onto the <i>P. mume</i> genome using TBLASTN, with an E-value cutoff of 1e-5, and homologous genomic sequences were aligned against matching proteins using GeneWise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Birney E., Clamp M. &amp; Durbin R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988–995 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2500">46</a></sup> for accurate spliced alignments.</p><p>In the EST-based prediction, 4,699 ESTs of <i>P. mume</i> were aligned against the <i>P. mume</i> genome using BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Kent W. J. BLAT--the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2513">42</a></sup> (identity ≥0.95 and coverage ≥0.90) to generate spliced alignments. The <i>de novo</i> set (28,610 to 36,095), four homologue-based results (24,277 to 29,586) and EST-based gene set (2001) were combined by GLEAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Elsik C. G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2520">47</a></sup> to integrate a consensus gene set. Short genes (CDS length &lt;150 bp) and low-quality genes (gaps in more than 10% of the coding region) were filtered. To finalize the gene set, we aligned RNA-Seq data from bud, fruit, leaf, root and stem to the genome using Tophat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Trapnell C., Pachter L. &amp; Salzberg S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105–1111 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2525">48</a></sup> (Version 1.2.0, implemented with bowtie1 Version 0.12.5), and the alignments were used as input for Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Trapnell C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 511–515 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2529">49</a></sup> (Version 0.93) with default parameters. Open reading frames of those transcripts were predicted using structure parameters trained on perfect genes from homology-based prediction. In the end, based on their coordinates on the genome sequences, we manually combined the Glean gene set and open reading frames of transcripts to form the final gene set that contains 31,390 genes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Comparative genome analyses</h3><p>Paralogues and orthologues genes were identified by BLASTP search (E-value cutoff of 1e-5). After removing self-matches, syntenic blocks (≥5 genes per block) were identified, based on MCscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Tang H. et al. Unraveling ancient hexaploidy through multiply-aligned angiosperm gene maps. Genome Res. 18, 1944–1954 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2541">19</a></sup>. The aligned results were used to generate dot plots—for self-aligned results, each block represents the paralogues region that arose from genome duplication, and for inter-aligned results, each block represents the orthologous region that was derived from a common ancestor. We calculated 4DTv for each gene pair in the block and drew the distribution of 4DTv values to estimate the speciation between species or WGD events.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Identification of duplicate and syntenic regions</h3><p>Three new parameters, defined in Salse <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Salse J., Abrouk M., Murat F., Quraishi U. M. &amp; Feuillet C. Improved criteria and comparative genomics tool provide new insights into grass paleogenomics. Brief Bioinform. 10, 619–630 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2555">50</a></sup>, were used to identify paralogous and orthologous relationships between <i>P. mume</i>, <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i>, <i>F. ve</i>sca and <i>V. vinifera</i> by BLASTN. Paralogous gene pairs that were identified during duplication analysis in <i>P. mume</i> and <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica,</i> respectively, and orthologous gene pairs that were identified by colinearity analysis between <i>P. mume</i> and <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i>, <i>F. ve</i>sca and <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i>, and <i>P. mume</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> were validated by CloseUp<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Hampson S. E., Gaut B. S. &amp; Baldi P. Statistical detection of chromosomal homology using shared-gene density alone. Bioinformatics 21, 1339–1348 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms2290#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d46598e2610">51</a></sup> analysis. Based on the syntenic and duplicate relationships, Rosaceae ancestral chromosomes were reconstructed, and the paleo-history was analysed.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec19" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec19-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec19">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec19-content"><p><b>How to cite this article:</b> Zhang, Q. <i>et al.</i> The genome of <i>Prunus mume</i>. <i>Nat. Commun.</i> 3:1318 doi: 10.1038/ncomms.2290 (2012).</p><p><b>Accession codes:</b> <i>P. mume</i> genome sequencing data have been deposited at NCBI BioProject under accession PRJNA171605. Sequencing data for <i>P. mume</i> and RAD analyses are available in the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (SRA) under accessions <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA056478">SRA056478</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA057102">SRA057102</a>. <i>P. mume</i> RNAseq data are available in the NCBI Gene Expression Omnibus (GEO) under accessions <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/query/acc.cgi?acc=GSE40162">GSE40162</a>. Full assembly and analysis data are available at <a href="http://prunusmumegenome.bjfu.edu.cn">http://prunusmumegenome.bjfu.edu.cn</a>.</p></div></div></section>

                  LDscaff: LD-based scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies
                </a>
              </h3>

                    class="c-article-further-reading__authors-list js-list-authors-3 js-etal-collapsed" data-component-authors-activator="authors-list"
                    id="authors-3">
                  <li><span

              <p class="c-article-further-reading__journal-title" id="journal-name-3">
                <i>BMC Bioinformatics</i>
                (2020)
              </p>
            </li>
